class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=X-ray+Absorption+Spectroscopy&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_X-ray%20absorption%20spectroscopy">X-ray absorption spectroscopy</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="article_content"><div class="article_content-table hlFld-FullText"><div class="article_content-row"><div class="article_content-left" property="articleBody" style="width: 384px;"><div id="sec1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i2">Introduction</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have received great interest over the past decade because of low-cost and evenly distributed sodium resources on earth with high natural abundance. Lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) have played an important role in electrical energy storage (EES) and conquered the portable electronic industry with high energy and power densities. However, the adoption of LIBs is impeded for the use of large-scale applications such as grid storage where the consumption of lithium is high which raises enormous production cost. Thus, SIBs are considered a potential candidate for the use of large-scale applications such as stationary EES. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref1" aria-label="reference 1">(1)</a> Further, sodium is the next alkali metal to lithium with monovalent and possesses the similar intercalation electrochemistry to lithium in many electrode materials. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref1" aria-label="reference 1">(1)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">Recent studies on cathode materials for SIBs have shown satisfactory performance when compared to LIBs. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref2" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref2" aria-label="reference 2">(2)</a> Some compositions with highly abundant and low-cost materials based on Fe and Mn (layered cathodes) are found to be electrochemically more active when tested in SIBs compare to LIBs. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref3" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref3" aria-label="reference 3">(3)</a> The major obstacle for the realization of SIBs is the development of the high-performance anode material. Graphite, which is the commonly used anode material for commercial LIBs, does not intercalate much sodium ions to use for SIBs. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref3" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref3" aria-label="reference 3">(3)</a> Silicon is broadly investigated as a high capacity anode material in LIBs but it is found electrochemically inactive in SIBs. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref4" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref4" aria-label="reference 4">(4)</a> The use of sodium metal is also not favorable for the practical use because of its low melting temperature (98 °C) and dendrite formation. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref5" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref5" aria-label="reference 5">(5)</a> The anode materials for SIBs can be classified according to de/sodiation processes into three different types which are categorized as de/alloying, conversion, and de/intercalation mechanism. Alloying-based anodes exhibit high capacity such as Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>Sn (295 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>), <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref6" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref6" aria-label="reference 6">(6)</a> Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>Sb (423 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>), <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref7" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref7" aria-label="reference 7">(7)</a> Na<sub>15</sub>Pb<sub>4</sub> (485 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>), <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref8" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref8" aria-label="reference 8">(8)</a> Na<sub>15</sub>Ge<sub>4</sub> (350 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>), <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref9" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref9" aria-label="reference 9">(9)</a> and so forth, however, these materials face the problem of rapid capacity fade due to large volume expansion. Conversion reaction-based materials also exhibit high capacity such as Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> (420 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>), <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref10" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref10" aria-label="reference 10">(10)</a> Co<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> (380 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>), <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref11" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref11" aria-label="reference 11">(11)</a> and so forth. The conversion reaction-based materials also suffer from limited cycle life because of the irreversible Na<sub>2</sub>O phase. Intercalation-based materials show limited capacity such as TiO<sub>2</sub> (130 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>), <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref12" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref12" aria-label="reference 12">(12)</a> Li<sub>4</sub>Ti<sub>5</sub>O<sub>12</sub> (120 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>), <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref13" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref13" aria-label="reference 13">(13)</a> NaFeTiO<sub>4</sub> (60 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>), <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref14" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref14" aria-label="reference 14">(14)</a> NaTiO<sub>2</sub> (152 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>), <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref15" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref15" aria-label="reference 15">(15)</a> NaTi<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (201 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>), <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref16" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref16" aria-label="reference 16">(16)</a> and so forth. Most of the abovementioned intercalation-based materials face the problem of rapid capacity fade over long cycling because of the larger ionic radius of sodium ions compared to lithium ions. Graphene and other carbon-based materials such as hard carbon show a capacity of &lt;300 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>, however, the large ionic size of sodium induces large structural changes which result in limited cycle life. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref17" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref17" aria-label="reference 17">(17)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">High capacity anode materials are required for the realization of SIBs and to fulfill the increasing demand of EES. In this regard, dual metal oxides (ABO<sub>4</sub> where A, B = metals, and O = oxygen) are good candidates for anode materials because they can deliver high capacities using multiple redox reactions. Dual metal oxide materials have shown high capacity through conversion reaction, high rate, and long cycle performance in LIBs. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref18 ref19 ref20" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref18 ref19 ref20" aria-label="reference 18 to 20">(18−20)</a> Conversion reaction-based anodes possess the advantage of high capacities as the metals can use all the oxidation states compared to intercalation anodes where the metals usually use the single oxidation state. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref21" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref21" aria-label="reference 21">(21)</a> Further, the moderate working voltages of conversion-based reaction make them suitable for safety concern compared to commonly used graphite anodes. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref22" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref22" aria-label="reference 22">(22)</a> Dual metal oxide materials possess the characteristics of synergic effects of multiple transition metals which make them superior to single-transition metals oxide. Single metal oxides face the problems of low coulombic efficiency, unstable solid-electrolyte interphase (SEI) layer formation, and poor cyclability. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref18" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref18" aria-label="reference 18">(18)</a> Dual metal oxide materials are expected to overcome these issues as they contain mixed metal oxides and possess better electrical conductivity than single metal oxides which results in improved electrochemical performance. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref20" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref20" aria-label="reference 20">(20)</a> Co- and Mo-based oxide materials have been studied for different applications such as magnetic, photocatalytic, optical, and electrochemical properties because of their relatively low cost, environmentally benign, nontoxic, and stable structure characteristics. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref23 ref24" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref23 ref24" aria-label="reference 23 and 24">(23,24)</a> Further, CoMoO<sub>4</sub> shows better catalytic activity in nonaqueous media and it can store a high amount of Li/Na ions (980 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>) by offering conversion reaction where Co<sup>2+</sup> reduces to Co<sup>0</sup> and Mo<sup>6+</sup> reduces to Mo<sup>0</sup>. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref23 ref25" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref23 ref25" aria-label="reference 23 and 25">(23,25)</a></div><div class="NLM_p last">In this study, a monoclinic-type mixed metal oxide, CoMoO<sub>4</sub> is synthesized using a facile solution method. The material is tested as an anode for SIBs after calcination where it delivers an initial specific capacity of 410 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>. The kinetic study is conducted for the CoMoO<sub>4</sub> anode material and the sodium diffusion coefficient is calculated using the galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT). Further, the sodium insertion/extraction mechanism into the anode material is followed using ex situ X-ray diffraction (XRD), X-ray absorption spectroscopy (XAS), and transmission electron microscopy (TEM) and the results suggest the amorphous-like structure of the material after the reaction.</div></div><div id="sec2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i3">Experimental Section</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">CoMoO<sub>4</sub> nanorods are synthesized using a simple solution method. The aqueous solutions of CoCl<sub>2</sub>·6H<sub>2</sub>O (Sigma-Aldrich, 98%) and Na<sub>2</sub>MoO<sub>4</sub> (Sigma-Aldrich, &gt;98%) were prepared separately with the stoichiometric ratio. Then both the solutions were slowly mixed under vigorous stirring and kept on stirring for 2 h at room temperature to form the precipitates. The precipitates are collected by vacuum filtration and washed several times to remove impurities. The collected material is dried at 80 °C overnight and hand ground using a mortar and pestle.</div><div class="NLM_p">Temperature-dependent time-resolved XRD (TR-XRD) was performed with a diffractometer (R-AXIS IV++, Rigaku) at Korea Institute of Science and Technology (KIST). The material was filled in a glass capillary tube and subjected to the measurement in a temperature range of 27–700 °C. Mo Kα radiation (wavelength of 0.7107 Å) was used as an X-ray source and the data was recorded on an image-plate detector. The 2θ values were later converted to Cu Kα radiation (λ = 1.54 Å) for comparison with standard data. The XRD of the calcined powder was performed with the same procedure as mentioned above. The morphology of the material was observed with field emission scanning electron microscopy (SEM; NOVA NanoSEM200, FEI, USA) and microstructure was evaluated by high-resolution (HR) TEM (Tecnai G<sup>2</sup> F20 FEI). The porosity and specific area of CoMoO<sub>4</sub> were measured by nitrogen adsorption at 77 K using the Brunauer–Emmett–Teller (BET) method at a BEL instrument (BEL, Japan). Chemical composition investigations were acquired using X-ray photoelectron microscopy (XPS) with PHI 5000 VersaProbe (ULVAC-PHI). The measurements were taken using a monochromatic Al Kα X-ray source (1486.6 eV) with a spot size of 100 × 100 μm under high vacuum conditions (6.8 × 10<sup>–8</sup> pa). The C 1s peak (284.6 eV) was used for energy calibration.</div><div class="NLM_p">The electrochemical properties were measured using a coin-type cell (CR 2032). The working electrodes were prepared by mixing the CoMoO<sub>4</sub> powder as an active material, Super P to increase electrical conductivity, and polyvinylidene fluoride as a binder in a weight ratio of 7:2:1. <i>N</i>-Methyl-2-pyrrolidinone was used as a solvent to make slurry and electrodes were prepared by casting the slurry onto a pure Cu foil. The working electrodes were prepared with an average mass loading of ∼3 mg cm<sup>–2</sup> and a thickness of ∼35 μm. The cells were prepared using the CoMoO<sub>4</sub> electrode as a working, sodium metal foil as a counter electrode and a glass fiber as a separator. The electrolyte used to make cells was 1 M NaClO<sub>4</sub> in propylene carbonate (PC) with 2 wt % of fluoroethylene carbonate. All the cell assembly was carried out in the argon-filled glovebox (Mbraun Unilab, Germany) with a controlled environment (both H<sub>2</sub>O and O<sub>2</sub> &lt; 0.1 ppm). The cells were galvanostatically tested on a battery cycler (Maccor 4000) at room temperature.</div><div class="NLM_p">Ex situ characterizations were carried out at fully discharged and recharged potential states during the 1st galvanostatic cycle and the cells were disassembled in the argon-filled glovebox. The electrodes were collected and thoroughly washed with PC and dimethyl carbonate solutions to remove the residue of the electrolyte. The washed electrodes with the Cu foil were sealed in plastic bags for XRD and XAS measurements while the material is gently scratched from the Cu foil for ex situ TEM measurements. The obtained electrode materials were dispersed sonically for 30 min before conducting TEM.</div><div class="NLM_p last">Ex situ XRD measurements were conducted using the same procedure as stated above for the powder sample. Ex situ XAS was performed at 7D XAFS beamline of the Pohang light source (PLS-II) using a Si(111) double crystal monochromator and a bending magnetic source. The experiments were repeated at 8C Nano XAFS beamline of PLS-II. The data was collected in the transmission mode and the energy was scanned from 200 to 1000 eV above the Mo and Co K-edges. Mo and Co pure metallic foils were simultaneously measured with the electrode samples and used as references to calibrate the spectra. The background removal and normalization of XAS data were performed using ATHENA package. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref26" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref26" aria-label="reference 26">(26)</a> Ex situ TEM analysis of fully discharged and recharged electrode materials was carried out by applying a supersonic vibration to ensure fine dispersion of particles in ethanol. The solution was dropped onto carbon TEM grids and the measurements were taken on a HR TEM (STEM, Talos F200X).</div></div><div id="sec3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i4">Results and Discussion</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">The as-prepared purple colored material was subjected to temperature-dependent TR-XRD in order to identify the phase changes with temperature. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a> shows a stack of TR-XRD patterns of the as-prepared material in a temperature range of 27–700 °C. The XRD pattern of as-prepared material shows the hydrated CoMoO<sub>4</sub> phase which is stable up to a temperature of 341 °C. The diffraction peaks of the CoMoO<sub>4</sub>·<i>x</i>H<sub>2</sub>O phase of as-prepared powder are matched with the previous report. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref27" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref27" aria-label="reference 27">(27)</a> The CoMoO<sub>4</sub>·<i>x</i>H<sub>2</sub>O phase suddenly vanishes when the temperature is raised to &gt;342 °C and CoMoO<sub>4</sub> phase is formed. However, the crystallinity improves with the rise in temperature but the diffraction peaks appear with less intensity when the temperature is further increased to 700 °C. The material has shown sharp diffraction peaks at ∼600 °C. Overall, the TR-XRD results show two major phases in a temperature range of 27–700 °C.</div><figure id="fig1" data-index="1" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b16324/asset/images/medium/am-2018-16324v_0001.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b16324/asset/images/large/am-2018-16324v_0001.jpeg" id="gr1" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 1. Temperature-dependent TR-XRD patterns of as prepared powder. The measurements were conducted at room temperature to 700 °C and the 2θ range is plotted between 17 and 60°.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b16324/asset/images/large/am-2018-16324v_0001.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.8b16324&amp;id=fig1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">After analyzing the TR-XRD results, it was observed that the sample shows sharp peaks at ∼600 °C. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>a shows the XRD pattern of the material calcined at 600 °C for 2 h in air to obtain phase pure material with better crystallinity. All the diffraction peaks belong to the monoclinic CoMoO<sub>4</sub> phase with a space group of <i>C</i>2/<i>m</i> and the peaks are matched with PDF card # 021-0868. The lattice parameters of calcined CoMoO<sub>4</sub> with monoclinic structure are calculated to be <i>a</i> = 11.07752 Å, <i>b</i> = 10.14019 Å, <i>c</i> = 6.73580 Å, β = 102.72027,° and <i>V</i> = 738.0499 Å<sup>3</sup>. The crystallite size is calculated using the Scherrer formula from three major peaks of (001), (021), and (002). The average crystallite size was estimated to be 21 nm for calcined CoMoO<sub>4</sub>. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>b shows the SEM images of the CoMoO<sub>4</sub> powder where most of the particles are observed with rodlike morphology with a length of &lt;1 μm and width of ∼100 nm along with small particles in the range of 100–200 nm. Further, the rods show linkage morphology where they are connected to each other in both horizontal and vertical directions.</div><figure id="fig2" data-index="2" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 2</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b16324/asset/images/medium/am-2018-16324v_0002.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b16324/asset/images/large/am-2018-16324v_0002.jpeg" id="gr2" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 2. (a) XRD pattern and (b) SEM image of calcined CoMoO<sub>4</sub> powder.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b16324/asset/images/large/am-2018-16324v_0002.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.8b16324&amp;id=fig2"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p"><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>a shows the TEM image of CoMoO<sub>4</sub> powder where the particles are oriented in random directions, however, most of the particles are observed with a rodlike morphology. The HR TEM image (taken of <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>a at arrow point) clearly shows lattice fringes and the monoclinic structure is marked (with red) on the relative atomic positions as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>b, indicating high crystallinity of the material. The inset of <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>b shows the selected area electron diffraction (SAED) pattern of CoMoO<sub>4</sub> and the reflections from (001), (021), and (022) planes are marked with arrows on the pattern. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>c shows the adsorption–desorption curves measured using nitrogen physisorption method. The BET method was employed to calculate the specific surface area and porosity in the material and they are calculated to be 9.0255 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>–1</sup> and 2.0736 cm<sup>3</sup> g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively. The Barrett–Joyner–Halenda pore size distribution curve shows the pore diameter of the rods is between 3 and 12 nm. The large porosity of the material is expected to be beneficial for electrolyte penetration into the interior of particles and facilitate the electrochemical reaction.</div><figure id="fig3" data-index="3" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 3</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b16324/asset/images/medium/am-2018-16324v_0003.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b16324/asset/images/large/am-2018-16324v_0003.jpeg" id="gr3" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 3. TEM images of CoMoO<sub>4</sub> powder at (a) low magnification showing rodlike morphology and at (b) high magnification with the monoclinic structure. The inset shows SAED pattern of CoMoO<sub>4</sub> powder. (c) BET surface area of CoMoO<sub>4</sub> powder and the inset shows the pore diameter.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b16324/asset/images/large/am-2018-16324v_0003.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.8b16324&amp;id=fig3"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The chemical composition of CoMoO<sub>4</sub> was obtained by performing XPS and <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>a shows the resulting XPS survey spectrum where it contains prominent peaks from cobalt (Co), oxygen (O), and molybdenum (Mo). <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>b shows the core-level peaks at binding energies of 780.2 and 796.5 eV which correspond to Co 2p<sub>3/2</sub> and Co 2p<sub>1/2</sub>, respectively and these energy values are typically observed for the Co<sup>2+</sup> oxidation state. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref28" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref28" aria-label="reference 28">(28)</a> The presence of other peaks at 782.34 and 798.83 eV could be observed in the fitted spectra of Co 2p which are attributed to the surface impurity compound. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref29" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref29" aria-label="reference 29">(29)</a> In the fitted spectrum of Co, peaks from Co(CO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>0.35</sub>Cl<sub>0.2</sub>(OH)<sub>1.1</sub> were also observed which could be because of growth on the surface. Co(CO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>0.35</sub>Cl<sub>0.2</sub>(OH)<sub>1.1</sub> is widely used as a precursor for the growth of Co<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> nanowires. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref30 ref31" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref30 ref31" aria-label="reference 30 and 31">(30,31)</a> Further, no traces of this impurity phase was observed in XRD, confirming the growth of CoMoO<sub>4</sub> as a major crystalline phase. The binding energy at 529.79 eV corresponds to O 1s of CoMoO<sub>4</sub> as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>c and the satellite peak at 531.70 eV is attributed to the surface impurity as observed in the Co 2p edge. Two regions were noticed in the case of Mo which relates to Mo 3d and Mo 3p. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>d shows the peaks at 231.73 and 234.89 eV which correspond to the Mo 3d<sub>5/2</sub> and Mo 3d<sub>3/2</sub>, respectively, indicating the Mo<sup>6+</sup> oxidation state. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref28" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref28" aria-label="reference 28">(28)</a> The peaks at 397.94 and 415.45 eV belongs to Mo 3p<sub>3/2</sub> and Mo 3p<sub>1/2</sub>, respectively and in good agreement to those reported for Mo<sup>6+</sup> oxidation state. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref32" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref32" aria-label="reference 32">(32)</a> Hence, the oxidation states of Co and Mo also confirms the formation of the CoMoO<sub>4</sub> phase with Co<sup>2+</sup> and Mo<sup>6+</sup>. Further, elemental analysis was also conducted by XPS and the atomic ratio was noticed 0.98:1 for Co and Mo, respectively which is close to the stoichiometry of CoMoO<sub>4</sub>.</div><figure id="fig4" data-index="4" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 4</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b16324/asset/images/medium/am-2018-16324v_0004.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b16324/asset/images/large/am-2018-16324v_0004.jpeg" id="gr4" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 4. (a) XPS survey spectrum of CoMoO<sub>4</sub> labeled with corresponding peaks. Fitted XPS spectra of (b) Co 2p, (c) O 1s, (d) Mo 3d, and (e) Mo 3p peaks.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b16324/asset/images/large/am-2018-16324v_0004.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.8b16324&amp;id=fig4"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">In order to observe the redox reaction, cyclic voltammetry (CV) was conducted in a potential range of 0.005–2.7 V as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>a. During first cathodic scan, three reduction peaks were observed at 0.7, 0.56, and 0.17 V which could be attributed to the sodium insertion into material and subsequent formation of the amorphous-like phase, conversion reaction where respective metallic phases are formed, and formation of irreversible decomposition of solvent to form the SEI layer. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref28" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref28" aria-label="reference 28">(28)</a> The first anodic scan reveals two oxidation peaks at 0.8 and 1.5 V which could be attributed to the oxidation of Co and Mo into CoO and MoO<sub>3</sub>. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref33 ref34 ref35" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref33 ref34 ref35" aria-label="reference 33 to 35">(33−35)</a> The 2nd cathodic scan reveals three reduction peaks at 0.95, 0.6, and 0.11 V which could be attributed to the sodium insertion into amorphous, CoO, and MoO<sub>3</sub> phases. The 2nd anodic scan shows similar features as the 1st scan with a slight shift of peaks and the peaks correspond to the oxidation of Co and Mo. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>b shows galvanostatic charge–discharge profiles of the CoMoO<sub>4</sub> electrode in 1st, 2nd, and 10th cycle at a current density of 49 mA g<sup>–1</sup> in a voltage range of 0.005–2.7 V. The open circuit potential was recorded to 1.6 V and as the sodiation process starts, the potential drops to ∼1.0 V and the discharge profile (sodiation) shows two plateaus at 0.95 and 0.75 V then the process follows a sloping profile. The plateaus could be attributed to the reduction of Mo<sup>6+</sup> and Co<sup>2+</sup> to their respective metals as shown by <a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq1" aria-label="eq 1">eq </a><a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq1" aria-label="1">1</a> and discussed in the CV part. The electrode exhibits a discharge capacity of 537 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> in the 1st cycle. In the reverse process, during charging (desodiation process), the material delivers a capacity of 410 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>, corresponding to a coulombic efficiency of 76%. The low value of coulombic efficiency during the 1st cycle is attributed to the partially irreversible formation of the SEI layer in metal oxides. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref36" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref36" aria-label="reference 36">(36)</a> The electrode shows different discharge–charge profiles in the subsequent cycles which are due to the formation of different reaction products as indicated by <a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq2" aria-label="eq 2">eq </a><a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq2" aria-label="2">2</a>.<span class="NLM_disp-formula"><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq1"><div class="equation"><img class="imgWithoutFigViewer " src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b16324/asset/images/medium/am-2018-16324v_m001.gif" alt="" id="_i5"></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(1)</span></div></div></span><span class="NLM_disp-formula"><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq2"><div class="equation"><img class="imgWithoutFigViewer " src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b16324/asset/images/medium/am-2018-16324v_m002.gif" alt="" id="_i6"></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(2)</span></div></div></span></div><figure id="fig5" data-index="5" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 5</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b16324/asset/images/medium/am-2018-16324v_0005.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b16324/asset/images/large/am-2018-16324v_0005.jpeg" id="gr5" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 5. (a) CV of CoMoO<sub>4</sub> electrode at a scan rate of 0.5 mV s<sup>–1</sup>. (b) Galvanostatic charge–discharge profile of the CoMoO<sub>4</sub> electrode at the 1st, 2nd, and 10th cycles. (c) Cycle test of the CoMoO<sub>4</sub> electrode at a current density of 49 mA g<sup>–1</sup>. (d) Rate capability test of the CoMoO<sub>4</sub> electrode in a potential window of 0.005–2.7 V.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b16324/asset/images/large/am-2018-16324v_0005.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.8b16324&amp;id=fig5"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The electrode exhibits discharge and charge capacities of 417 and 377 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively during the 2nd cycle, corresponding to a coulombic efficiency of &gt;90%. The coulombic efficiency significantly improved in the subsequent cycles and the electrode shows a coulombic efficiency of &gt;98% at the 10th cycle where it delivers discharge and charge capacities of 351 and 346 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively. Moreover, the similar potential profiles during 2nd and 10th cycles indicate that the material repeatedly follows the reaction as indicated by <a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq2" aria-label="eq 2">eq </a><a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq2" aria-label="2">2</a>. A high coulombic efficiency of &gt;97% was retained by the electrode during long cycling and the electrode shows a coulombic efficiency of 99% at the 100th cycle as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>c. The CoMoO<sub>4</sub> anode shows higher capacities compared to other Mo-based anodes for SIBs such as BaMoO<sub>4</sub> delivered specific capacities of 121 and 50 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> during the 1st and 100th cycle, respectively. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref37" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref37" aria-label="reference 37">(37)</a> Further, the CoMoO<sub>4</sub> anode delivers capacities comparable or higher than single metal oxides such as MoO<sub>3</sub> (298 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 1st cycle), <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref38" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref38" aria-label="reference 38">(38)</a> MoO<sub>3</sub>/C (335 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 1st cycle), <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref39" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref39" aria-label="reference 39">(39)</a> Co<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> (327 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 1st cycle), <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref40" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref40" aria-label="reference 40">(40)</a> nano Co<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> (168 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 1st cycle), <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref41" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref41" aria-label="reference 41">(41)</a> ZnSnO<sub>3</sub> (315 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 1st cycle), <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref42" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref42" aria-label="reference 42">(42)</a> and NaVO<sub>3</sub> (196 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 1st cycle). <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref43" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref43" aria-label="reference 43">(43)</a> The rate capability (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>d) of the CoMoO<sub>4</sub> electrode was measured in a potential range of 0.005–2.7 V at current densities of 49, 98, 245, 490, and 980 mA g<sup>–1</sup> where the electrode delivered average specific capacities of 360, 317, 274, 221, and 165 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively. The electrode recovers a specific capacity of 266 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> when current density reset to 49 mA g<sup>–1</sup> after deep cycling. Hence, the electrode shows good rate capability.</div><div class="NLM_p">In order to follow the sodium diffusivity into CoMoO<sub>4</sub>, we have conducted a GITT during sodium insertion/extraction. GITT is the most reliable method to follow sodium kinetics into the electrode as it is based on chronopotentiometry at nearly thermodynamic equilibrium and to calculate the diffusion coefficient at various potential points with highly resolved data. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref44 ref45" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref44 ref45" aria-label="reference 44 and 45">(44,45)</a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a> shows the potential response profile of the CoMoO<sub>4</sub> anode material as a function of time with the sodium insertion/extraction process. The cell was cycled at a rate of 49 mA g<sup>–1</sup> in a potential range of 0.005–2.7 V and a current pulse was applied for 10 min with a subsequent relaxing time for 1 h to reach equilibrium potential state. This procedure was repeated for a complete discharge and recharge process. The electrode potential is influenced by each current pulse because of insertion/extraction of sodium ions into the CoMoO<sub>4</sub> electrode. The inset of <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a> shows a single step of applied current with an interpretation of parameters during the sodium insertion process. The values of each parameter are recorded and used to calculate the sodium diffusion coefficient (<i>D</i><sub>Na</sub>) for each step. <i>D</i><sub>Na</sub> was calculated using <a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq3" aria-label="eq 3">eq </a><a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq3" aria-label="3">3</a> by assuming that sodium transport obeys Fick’s law. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref46" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref46" aria-label="reference 46">(46)</a><span class="NLM_disp-formula"><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq3"><div class="equation"><img class="imgWithoutFigViewer " src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b16324/asset/images/medium/am-2018-16324v_m003.gif" alt="" id="_i7"></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(3)</span></div></div></span>where τ is the pulse time during applied current density, <i>m</i><sub>B</sub> (g) is the weight loading, <i>V</i><sub>M</sub> (cm<sup>3</sup> mol<sup>–1</sup>) is the molar volume, <i>M</i><sub>B</sub> (g mol<sup>–1</sup>) is the molecular weight, <i>S</i> (cm<sup>2</sup>) is the effective surface area of the electrode, Δ<i>E</i><sub>s</sub> is the change in steady state potential for the step, and Δ<i>E</i><sub>τ</sub> is the total change in the cell potential during the current pulse for the time τ. The effective surface area was used as measured by the BET method and the value of <i>V</i><sub>M</sub> is assumed to be constant during calculations. <i>D</i><sub>Na</sub> was calculated to be 1.565 × 10<sup>–15</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>–1</sup> at the start and 4.447 × 10<sup>–18</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>–1</sup> at the end of the discharge process and 4.37 × 10<sup>–16</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>–1</sup> at the start and 1.77 × 10<sup>–18</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>–1</sup> at the end of the charge process. The calculated values of <i>D</i><sub>Na</sub> show that sodium ions faced high polarization toward the depth of discharge and charge process which are related to the sluggish kinetics into the electrode. The reason for sluggish kinetics is mainly attributed to the formation of different phases during conversion reaction which creates high kinetic barriers due to structural rearrangements. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref44" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref44" aria-label="reference 44">(44)</a> The calculated <i>D</i><sub>Na</sub> values for CoMoO<sub>4</sub> are comparable to the reported Na<sub>4</sub>Mn<sub>9</sub>O<sub>18</sub> (10<sup>–17</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>–1</sup>), <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref47" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref47" aria-label="reference 47">(47)</a> and lower than the NaVO<sub>3</sub> (10<sup>–13</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>–1</sup>), <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref43" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref43" aria-label="reference 43">(43)</a> and Na<sub>2</sub>Ti<sub>3</sub>O<sub>7</sub> (10<sup>–12</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>–1</sup>). <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref48" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref48" aria-label="reference 48">(48)</a></div><figure id="fig6" data-index="6" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 6</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b16324/asset/images/medium/am-2018-16324v_0006.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b16324/asset/images/large/am-2018-16324v_0006.jpeg" id="gr6" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 6. Galvanostatic charge–discharge profile of GITT measurement in a potential range of 0.005–2.7 V along with the calculated sodium diffusion coefficient into the CoMoO<sub>4</sub> electrode. The inset shows a magnified single step during the discharge process labeled with variables.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b16324/asset/images/large/am-2018-16324v_0006.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.8b16324&amp;id=fig6"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">Probing the reaction mechanism is an important factor for electrode materials to observe and improve the electrochemical performance. Herein, we have investigated the sodium insertion/extraction mechanism using ex situ techniques such as XRD, XAS, and TEM. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">7</a>a shows the XRD patterns of pristine, fully discharged, and recharged electrodes. The XRD peaks in the pristine electrode are well-matched with the calcined material as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>a. No obvious diffraction peak was detected in both XRD patterns and the material shows transformation to the amorphous-like structure after sodium insertion. Further, X-ray absorption near edge structure (XANES) spectroscopy was performed to observe the capacity contribution in the reaction. XANES is a powerful technique to probe the electronic structure of the material and the data is collected using the transmitted X-ray beam which passes through the electrode sample. Thus, the information obtained from XANES is more reliable for ex situ electrode materials compared to XPS and other surface techniques. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">7</a>b shows the Mo K-edge XANES spectra of pristine, fully discharged, fully recharged CoMoO<sub>4</sub> electrode, and standard Mo metallic foil. The XANES spectrum of pristine shows a high pre-edge peak and its amplitude depends on the degree of Mo–O bond’s distortion and that of the 4d(Mo)–2p(O) hybridization. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref49" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref49" aria-label="reference 49">(49)</a> The XANES spectrum of fully discharged electrode shows a shift toward lower energy and almost overlaps the Mo metallic spectrum at the lower intensity region, indicating a reduction in the Mo oxidation state. However, the upper half part of the fully discharged XANES spectrum is away from the Mo metallic spectrum which is probably because of insufficient reduction of Mo ions. Upon fully recharged, the XANES spectrum shows a reverse shift toward higher energy, indicating oxidation of Mo. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">7</a>c shows the XANES spectra of the Co K-edge of pristine, fully discharged, fully recharged electrode, and standard Co metallic foil. The XANES spectrum of pristine electrode shows a less intense pre-edge peak which is an indication of less distorted Co–O octahedral symmetry. When the electrode is fully discharged, the XANES spectrum shows a significant shift to lower energy, indicating the reduction from Co<sup>2+</sup> toward the Co<sup>0</sup> state. The fully discharged spectra of both Mo and Co do not overlap the metallic foils which are mainly because of incomplete reduction of both metals which was also evidenced by the low specific capacity of the material. The XANES spectrum of the fully recharged electrode shows a shift to higher energy which is the indication of oxidation of Co. Hence, both metal species (Mo and Co) participate in the reversible electrochemical reaction.</div><figure id="fig7" data-index="7" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 7</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b16324/asset/images/medium/am-2018-16324v_0007.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b16324/asset/images/large/am-2018-16324v_0007.jpeg" id="gr7" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 7. (a) Ex situ XRD of pristine, fully discharged and recharged CoMoO<sub>4</sub> electrode. XANES spectra of pristine, fully discharged and recharged electrodes along with metallic foils of (b) Mo K-edge and (c) Co K-edge. EXAFS spectra of pristine (CoMoO<sub>4</sub>), fully discharged and recharged electrodes along references and metallic foil of (d) Co and (e) Mo edge. (f) Raman spectra of pristine, fully discharged and recharged electrodes.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b16324/asset/images/large/am-2018-16324v_0007.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.8b16324&amp;id=fig7"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">Ex situ XRD results of fully discharged and recharged electrodes show the amorphous-like structure of the materials and XANES spectra show incomplete reduction into Co and Mo metallic phases. Thus, in order to observe the local structure, the extended X-ray absorption fine structure (EXAFS) is used to determine the short-range order in the materials. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">7</a>d,e shows the EXAFS of pristine CoMoO<sub>4</sub>, fully discharged, and recharged along with references and metallic foils of Co and Mo edges, respectively. EXAFS of pristine CoMoO<sub>4</sub> at Co edge shows clear peaks of the Co–O interaction at 1.54 Å and Co–Co interaction peak at ∼2.5 Å. In the fully discharged spectrum of the Co edge, the first peak of Co–O at 1.54 Å could be observed with relatively low intensity, whereas a new peak emerged at 2.16 Å (Co–Co interaction) which belongs to the Co metallic foil. This confirms the conversion to the metallic Co phase in the fully discharged electrode. The fully recharged spectrum at the Co edge shows peaks at 1.54 and 2.5 Å which correspond to the Co–O interaction. The peak at 2.5 Å in the fully recharged EXAFS spectrum matches to the CoO phase as indicated in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">7</a>d. The Mo K-edge EXAFS spectrum of pristine CoMoO<sub>4</sub> shows a prominent peak at 1.25 Å which corresponds to the Mo–O interaction. The fully discharged EXAFS spectrum at the Mo edge retains the first peak and an additional peak appears at ∼2.5 Å, corresponding to the Mo–Mo interaction of the Mo metallic foil as indicated in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>e. The EXAFS spectrum at the Mo edge of the recharged electrode shows peaks at 1.2 and 1.7 Å, corresponding to Co–O–O interactions which are close to the peaks of reference MoO<sub>3</sub> (1.15 and 1.63 Å). Thus, EXAFS results confirm the conversion reaction in the CoMoO<sub>4</sub> electrode.</div><div class="NLM_p"><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">7</a>f shows Raman spectra of pristine, fully discharged, and recharged electrode. The pristine spectrum shows three major peaks at 809, 876, and 930 cm<sup>–1</sup> and can be assigned to Mo–O–Co vibrations in CoMoO<sub>4</sub>. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref50" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref50" aria-label="reference 50">(50)</a> The pristine spectrum also shows peaks from D and G bands of carbon in the electrodes. The vibrations from CoMoO<sub>4</sub> are hardly detected in fully discharged and recharged electrode. However, a new peak at 678 cm<sup>–1</sup> was observed in both samples. This peak can be assigned to the Na–O stretching vibration. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref51" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref51" aria-label="reference 51">(51)</a> Thus, Raman spectroscopy results also confirm the formation of the amorphous-like behavior of the material after sodiation.</div><div class="NLM_p">The ex situ XRD results showed the amorphous-like phase of fully discharged and recharged electrode. In order to further gain insight into microstructure and element distribution, ex situ TEM of the fully discharged and recharged electrode material was conducted. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig8" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig8" id="" class=" internalNav">8</a>a shows the HR TEM image of the fully discharged electrode. The rodlike morphology of the material was retained after the sodiation process, however, no clear lattice fringes were observed in the HR-TEM images as shown in the inset of the <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig8" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig8" id="" class=" internalNav">8</a>a, indicating amorphous-like structure as also evidenced by ex situ XRD results. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig8" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig8" id="" class=" internalNav">8</a>b shows the high angle annular dark field (HAADF) image of rod and <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig8" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig8" id="" class=" internalNav">8</a>c–f show electron energy dispersive spectroscopy (EDS) images. The EDS images of Co, O, Mo, and Na reveal the homogenous distribution of respective elements.</div><figure id="fig8" data-index="8" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 8</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b16324/asset/images/medium/am-2018-16324v_0008.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b16324/asset/images/large/am-2018-16324v_0008.jpeg" id="gr8" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 8. (a) Ex situ TEM image of the fully discharged electrode and the inset shows the HR image of the particle. (b) HAADF image of rodlike morphology and the corresponding EDS images of (c) Co, (d) O, (e) Mo, and (f) Na elements.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b16324/asset/images/large/am-2018-16324v_0008.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.8b16324&amp;id=fig8"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p"><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig9" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig9" id="" class=" internalNav">9</a>a shows ex situ TEM image of a rodlike particle of the fully recharged electrode. The particle retains morphology but no sign of lattice fringes was observed as shown in the inset of the <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig9" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig9" id="" class=" internalNav">9</a>a. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig9" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig9" id="" class=" internalNav">9</a>b shows the HAADF image of a rod and <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig9" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig9" id="" class=" internalNav">9</a>c–f show the corresponding EDS images of Co, O, Mo, and Na, respectively. All the images show the homogeneous distribution of each element, however, the signals of Na is either due to irreversible amorphous Na<sub>2</sub>O or formation of SEI layer and/or sodium salt. Overall, ex situ TEM results reveal that the material retains its rodlike morphology, transforms into the amorphous-like structure and a homogeneous distribution of elements. The EDS images of discharged and recharged electrode show clear and larger Co-rich particles which are related to the higher electrochemical activity of cobalt in the sample. The unique rodlike morphology of the material which was retained after cycling shows stability and mechanical strength of the materials and is considered beneficial for electrochemical performance. The reaction products of CoO and MoO<sub>3</sub> have shown the synergistic effect where they assure a suitable electrical conductivity, resulting in improved electrochemical performance. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref23" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref23" aria-label="reference 23">(23)</a></div><figure id="fig9" data-index="9" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 9</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b16324/asset/images/medium/am-2018-16324v_0009.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b16324/asset/images/large/am-2018-16324v_0009.jpeg" id="gr9" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 9. (a) Ex situ TEM image of the fully recharged electrode and the inset shows the HR image of the particle. (b) HAADF image and the corresponding EDS images of (c) Co, (d) O, (e) Mo, and (f) Na elements.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b16324/asset/images/large/am-2018-16324v_0009.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.8b16324&amp;id=fig9"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure></div><div id="sec4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i8">Conclusions</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p last">Single phase CoMoO<sub>4</sub> with rodlike morphology was synthesized using a simple solution method. TR-XRD was conducted in order to find suitable calcination temperature and the material with high crystallinity was obtained at 600 °C as also evidenced by the HR-TEM image. The material delivers a specific capacity of 410 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at the 1st cycle and retains a capacity of 229 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> after the 100th cycle. The average specific capacities of 274, 221, and 165 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> were recorded when the material is tested under high current densities of 245, 490, and 980 mA g<sup>–1</sup>. The sodium diffusion coefficient was determined by the GIIT method and the values are calculated in the range of 1.565 × 10<sup>–15</sup> to 4.447 × 10<sup>–18</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>–1</sup> during galvanostatic cycling. The reaction mechanism investigations conducted by ex situ XRD, XAS and TEM show that the material transforms to the amorphous-like structure, charge compensation is due to reversible redox of Mo and Co, and a homogenous distribution of elements in discharged and recharged products, respectively. Hence, the results presented in this study will pave the way for the development of mixed-metal oxides as an anode for SIBs.</div></div><div class="NLM_back"><div class="article_supporting-info" id="_suppInfo"></div><div class="testing" data-doi="10.1021/acsami.8b16324" data-url="https://widgets.figshare.com/static/figshare.js" id="figshare-widget" style="display: block;"></div><div class="authorInformationSection"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="authorInformationSection">Author Information</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><ul><li><span class="author-information-subsection-header">Corresponding Author</span><ul><li><div class="authorItemInformation"><span class="hlFld-ContribAuthor strong">Kyung Yoon Chung</span> - <span class="hlFld-Affiliation affiliation">Center

                        cas_cit2R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">2</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">A phosphorene-graphene hybrid material as a high-capacity anode for sodium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Sun, Jie; Lee, Hyun-Wook; Pasta, Mauro; Yuan, Hongtao; Zheng, Guangyuan; Sun, Yongming; Li, Yuzhang; Cui, Yi</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nature Nanotechnology</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2015</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">10</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">11</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">980-985</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">NNAABX</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1748-3387</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Nature Publishing Group</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Na-ion batteries have recently attracted significant attention as an alternative to Li-ion batteries because Na sources do not present the geopolitical issues that Li sources might.  Although recent reports on cathode materials for Na-ion batteries demonstrated performances comparable to their Li-ion counterparts, the major scientific challenge for a competitive Na-ion battery technol. is to develop viable anode materials.  A hybrid material made out of a few phosphorene layers sandwiched between graphene layers shows a specific capacity of 2 440 mA h g-1 (calcd. using the mass of P only) at a c.d. of 0.05 A g-1 and an 83% capacity retention after 100 cycles while operating between 0 and 1.5 V.  Using in situ TEM and ex situ x-ray diffraction techniques, the authors explain the large capacity of the anode through a dual mechanism of intercalation of Na ions along the x axis of the phosphorene layers followed by the formation of a Na3P alloy.  The presence of graphene layers in the hybrid material works as a mech. backbone and an elec. highway, ensuring that a suitable elastic buffer space accommodates the anisotropic expansion of phosphorene layers along the y and z axial directions for stable cycling operation.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref3"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref3" class="refNumLink">3</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit3" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.201703268"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Saurel, D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Orayech, B.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xiao, B.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Carriazo, D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Rojo, T.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">From Charge Storage Mechanism to Performance: A Roadmap toward High Specific Energy Sodium-Ion Batteries through Carbon Anode Optimization</span>. <i>Adv.                         cas_cit4R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">4</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Elucidating the reaction mechanism of SnF2@C nanocomposite as a high-capacity anode material for Na-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Ali, Ghulam; Lee, Ji-Hoon; Oh, Si Hyoung; Jung, Hun-Gi; Chung, Kyung Yoon</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nano Energy</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2017</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">42</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue"></span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">106-114</span>CODEN:

        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">2211-2855</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier Ltd.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Sn-based materials have drawn great attention as anodes for rechargeable batteries because of their extremely high theor. energy storage capacities.  Herein, a nanocomposite based on SnF2 and acetylene black is proposed as a high-performance anode material for sodium-ion batteries and their electrochem. performances, as well as related energy storage mechanism, are investigated.  The nanocomposite electrode delivered a high reversible capacity of 563 mAh g-1 which is considerably improved compared to a reversible capacity of 323 mAh g-1 of the micron-sized bare SnF2 electrode.  The nanocomposite electrode shows superior rate capability and delivers a reversible capacity of 191 mAh g-1 at a high c.d. of 1 C, while the bare electrode delivers negligible capacities.  The changes in crystallog. structure are obsd. using in-situ XRD and the results reveal the existence of a solid soln. of two or more species during dis/charging.  The electronic and at. configurations depending on the state of dis/charging are systematically investigated using ex-situ X-ray absorption spectroscopy.  The results reveal that the valence change of Sn follows the conversion (SnF2 + 2Na → Sn + 2NaF) and alloying (Sn + XNa → SnNaX) reaction upon sodium insertion into a composite.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref5"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref5" class="refNumLink">5</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit5" data-doi="10.1039/c2ee02781j"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Palomares, V.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Serras, P.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Villaluenga, I.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hueso, K. Sci.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2012</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>5</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">5884</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">5901</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1039/c2ee02781j</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D5%26publication_year%3D2012%26pages%3D5884-5901%26journal%3DEnergy%2BEnviron.%2BSci.%26author%3DV.%2BPalomares%26author%3DP.%2BSerras%26author%3DI.%2BVillaluenga%26author%3DK.%2BB.%2BHueso%26author%3DJ.%2BCarretero-Gonz%25C3%25A1lez%26author%3DT.%2BRojo%26title%3DNa-Ion%2BBatteries%252C%2BRecent%2BAdvances%2Band%2BPresent%2BChallenges%2Bto%2BBecome%2BLow%2BCost%2BEnergy%2BStorage%2BSystems%26doi%3D10.1039%252Fc2ee02781j&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsami.8b16324&amp;doiOfLink=10.1039%2Fc2ee02781j&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit5R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">5</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Na-ion batteries, recent advances and present challenges to become low cost energy storage systems</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Palomares, Veronica; Serras, Paula; Villaluenga, Irune; Hueso, Karina B.; Carretero-Gonzalez, Javier; Rojo, Teofilo</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Energy &amp; Environmental Science</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2012</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">5</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">3</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">5884-5901</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">EESNBY</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1754-5706</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Energy prodn. and storage have become key issues concerning our welfare in daily life.  Present challenges for batteries are twofold.  In the first place, the increasing demand for powering systems of portable electronic devices and zero-emission vehicles stimulates research towards high energy and high voltage systems.  In the second place, low cost batteries are required in order to advance towards smart elec. grids that integrate discontinuous energy flow from renewable sources, optimizing the performance of clean energy sources.  Na-ion batteries can be the key for the second point, because of the huge availability of sodium, its low price and the similarity of both Li and Na insertion chemistries.  In spite of the lower energy d. and voltage of Na-ion based technologies, they can be focused on applications where the wt. and footprint requirement is less drastic, such as elec. grid storage.  Much work has to be done in the field of Na-ion in order to catch up with Li-ion technol.  Cathodic and anodic materials must be optimized, and new electrolytes will be the key point for Na-ion success.  This review will gather the up-to-date knowledge about Na-ion battery materials, with the aim of providing a wide view of the systems that have already been explored and a starting point for the new research on this battery technol.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref6"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref6" class="refNumLink">6</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit6" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.201200346"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xu, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhu, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, C.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Electrochemical Performance of Porous Carbon/Tin Composite Anodes for Sodium-Ion and Lithium-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>Adv.                         cas_cit7R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">7</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Sb/C composite as a high-performance anode for sodium ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Wang, Guanhua; Xiong, Xunhui; Lin, Zhihua; Yang, Chenghao; Lin, Zhang; Liu, Meilin</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Electrochimica Acta</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2017</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">242</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue"></span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">159-164</span>CODEN:

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier Ltd.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Na ion batteries (SIBs) were considered as promising alternative to Li ion batteries (LIBs) for large-scale energy storage.  However, their inferior electrochem. performances, esp. cyclability and rate capability become the major challenge for further development of SIBs.  Herein, Sb/C composite prepd. via an efficient and facile method with alginate as precursor was evaluated as anode material for Na ion batteries.  This method shows unique role in formation of porous Sb/C composite, controlling the particle size and achieving excellent C coating.  Such a composite demonstrates high specific capacity (423 mAh g-1 at 0.1 A g-1), good rate capability (226 mAh g-1 at 15 A g-1), and excellent cycle life (81.4% capacity retention for 200 cycles at 2 A g-1).  The facile synthesis and superior electrochem. performance of the Sb/C composite render it a promising anode material for high-performance SIBs.  Also, the authors' study shows a feasible and effective way to prep. transition metal (oxides) and porous C nanocomposite for SIBs.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref8"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref8" class="refNumLink">8</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit8" data-doi="10.1149/2.080403jes"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ellis, L.                         cas_cit8R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">8</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">In situ XRD study of silicon, lead and bismuth negative electrodes in nonaqueous sodium cells</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Ellis, L.         (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">3</span>),

        </div><div class="casAbstract">The electrochem. alloying of sodium with silicon, lead and bismuth was studied by in-situ X-ray diffraction.  No evidence was found for sodium insertion into silicon at temps. up to 60°C.  Lead was found to catalytically decomp. electrolyte, hindering sodiation.  This could be avoided by applying a high current pulse to the cell to sodiate the lead surface.  Once the surface was sodiated, further sodiation of lead could proceed at low currents.  The sodiation of lead followed a path that differs from the equil. phase diagram and from that described in earlier reports.  During sodiation, Na9Pb4 was formed with a previously unreported structure that was found to be isostructural with Na9Sn4.  It was found that Na could be reversibly inserted into bismuth.  The mechanism for bismuth sodiation follows equil. phase behavior.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref9"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref9" class="refNumLink">9</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit9" data-doi="10.1016/j.elecom.2013.05.025"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Baggetto, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Keum, J.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Germanium thin film electrodes show a reversible Na-ion reaction at potentials around 0.15/0.6 V.                         cas_cit11R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">11</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Shale-like Co3O4 for high performance lithium/sodium ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Li, Huan-Huan; Li, Zi-Yao; Wu, Xing-Long; Zhang, Lin-Lin; Fan, Chao-Ying; Wang, Hai-Feng; Li, Xiao-Ying; Wang, Kang; Sun, Hai-Zhu; Zhang, Jing-Ping</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Materials Chemistry A: Materials for Energy and Sustainability</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">In recent years, metal-org. compds. have been considered as ideal sacrificial templates to obtain transition metal oxides for electrochem. applications due to their diverse structures and tunable properties.  A new kind of Co-based metal org. compd. with a layered structure was designed and prepd., which was then transformed into ultrafine Co oxide (Co3O4) nanocrystallites via a facile annealing treatment.  The obtained Co3O4 nanocrystallites further assembled into a hierarchical shale-like structure, donating extremely short ion diffusion pathway and rich porosity to the materials.  The special structure largely alleviated the problems of Co3O4 such as inferior intrinsic elec. cond., poor ion transport kinetics and large vol. changes during the redox reactions.  When evaluated as anode materials for Li-ion batteries, the shale-like Co3O4 (S-Co3O4) exhibited superior Li storage properties with a high capacity of 1045.3 mA-h g-1 after 100 cycles at 200 mA g-1 and good rate capabilities up to 10 A g-1.  Also, the S-Co3O4 showed decent electrochem. performance in Na-ion batteries due to the above-mentioned comprehensive merits (380 and 153.8 mA-h g-1 at 50 and 5000 mA g-1, resp.).</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref12"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref12" class="refNumLink">12</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit12" data-doi="10.1039/c7nr01417a"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ata-Ur-Rehman, A.-U.-R.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ali, G.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Badshah, A.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chung, K.         </div><div class="casAbstract">In order to improve the electrochem. kinetics of anatase titania (TiO2), Mn-doped TiO2 incorporated with functionalized multiwall carbon nanotubes (MWCNTs) has been prepd. by a modified hydrothermal method and tested for both lithium (LIB) and sodium-ion battery (SIB) anodes.  The size of the TiO2 particles is controlled to ∼35-40 nm, with almost even distribution on the MWCNTs surface.  The nanostructuring and appropriate doping of cost-effective manganese into the TiO2 host improved the electrochem. performance in terms of high rate capability and specific capacity for both the rechargeable battery systems.  For the LIBs, the charge capacity of the 5% Mn-TiO2/MWCNT anode is 226.3 mA h g-1 in the first cycle, and is retained at 176.4 mA h g-1 after 80 cycles as compared with the SIBs, in which the charge capacity is 152.1 mA h g-1 in the first cycle, and is retained at 121.4 mA h g-1 after 80 cycles.  After testing the electrodes at a high current rate of 20C, the nanocomposite electrode can still demonstrate charge capacities of 131.2 and 117.2 mA h g-1 at a 0.1C rate for LIBs and SIBs, resp.  The incorporation of Mn-ions (2+, 4+) is found to play a crucial role in terms of defects and vacancy creation, increasing conduction band electrons and lattice expansion to facilitate alkali metal ion diffusion for superior electrochem. performance.  The combination of heteroatom doping and use of a highly conductive additive in the form of MWCNTs has resulted in excellent electrode integrity, high ion accessibility, and fast electron transport.  Its outstanding cycling stability and remarkable rate performance make the 5% Mn-TiO2/MWCNT a promising anode material for high-performance LIBs and SIBs.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref13"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref13" class="refNumLink">13</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit13" data-doi="10.1039/c7ta03173d"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hou, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Fan, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xia, Y.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Carbon-Coated Li4Ti5O12 Nanoparticles with High Electrochemical Performance as Anode Material in Sodium-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>J.                         cas_cit13R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">13</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Carbon-coated Li4Ti5O12 nanoparticles with high electrochemical performance as anode material in sodium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Liu, Yao; Liu, Jingyuan; Hou, Mengyan; Fan, Long; Wang, Yonggang; Xia, Yongyao</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Materials Chemistry A: Materials for Energy and Sustainability</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Sodium-ion batteries have been considered as promising alternatives to the current lithium-ion batteries owing to their low cost and abundant raw material.  The major challenge of their practical implementation is the lack of favorable anode material.  Spinel Li4Ti5O12 has been regarded as a potential anode material for its superior capability of sodium-ion storage and relatively appropriate operating voltage.  However, the low intrinsic ionic and electronic cond. of spinel Li4Ti5O12 still remains as its major drawback.
