                        cas_cit14R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">14</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Improving the sodium storage capacity of tunnel structured NaxFexTi2-xO4 (x =1, 0.9 &amp; 0.8) anode materials by tuning sodium deficiency</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Bhange, Deu S.; Ali, Ghulam; Kim, Ji-Young; Chung, Kyung Yoon; Nam, Kyung-Wan</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Power Sources</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Due to their abundance and environmentally benign nature, Fe and Ti present as the most attractive potential elements for use in rechargeable Na-ion batteries (SIBs).  Accordingly, 2 structurally different Fe and Ti based compds., stoichiometric NaFeTiO4 and Na deficient NaxFexTi2-xO4 (where x =0.9, and 0.8), are explored as anode materials for SIBs.  Their structure and sodium storage capacity are systematically studied using combined structural and electrochem. anal.  Rietveld refinement anal. reveals that the Na deficiency leads to the structural transformation from a single-tunnel structure (NaFeTiO4) to a zigzag-type double-tunnel structure (Na0.9Fe0.9Ti1.1O4 and Na0.8Fe0.8Ti1.2O4).  The series of Na deficient compds. bears systematic Na ion vacancies in their structure ≤20%.  Na deficiency in the NaxFexTi2-xO4 logically provides addnl. space for accommodating the excess Na ions as such the NaxFexTi2-xO4 compds. with higher level of Na deficiency show higher specific capacities than the stoichiometric NaFeTiO4.  All the compds. exhibited very good electrochem. cycling stability, with minimal capacity loss during cycling.  This approach is a model example of improvement in the Na storage capacity of the anode materials by tuning the chem. compn., and could facilitate the performance improvement of known or new electrode materials for SIBs.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref15"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref15" class="refNumLink">15</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit15" data-doi="10.1039/c4ee03045a"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wu, D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xu, B.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Twu, N.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ceder, G.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">NaTiO2: A Layered Anode Material for Sodium-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>Energy Environ.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Energy-storage devices are state-of-the-art devices with many potential tech. and domestic applications.  Conventionally used batteries do not meet the requirements of elec. or plug-in hybrid-elec. vehicles due to their insufficient energy and power densities.  Graphite is used for the conventional anodes of Li-ion batteries.  However, the specific capacity (372 mA h g-1) of a graphite electrode is not sufficient for high-power applications.  Therefore, Co-, Ni-, Mn-, and Zn-based simple oxides have been investigated as anode components due to their high specific capacities (500-1000 mA h g-1).  Among these, Co-based anodes have demonstrated the best electrochem. performances; however, Co's high cost and toxicity limit its use as an ideal anode component.  Recently, mixed-metal oxides with AB2O4 (A = Cu, Co, Ni, Mn, and Zn; B = Co, Mn, and Fe) and A2BO4 (A = Co, Mn, and Fe; B = Sn, Si, Ti, and Ge) structures have received much interest, and their electrochem. performances have been extensively studied.  This type of mixed-metal oxide affords the following main advantages: they store charge through conversion as well as alloying-dealloying processes, and they exhibit higher electronic conductivities than that obtained with simple metal oxides.  The above points indicate the importance of AB2O4- and A2BO4-structured materials.  The present review emphasizes the recent literature on the electrochem. performance of AB2O4- and A2BO4-structured materials and their composites and feasible ways to implement these materials in Li-ion batteries in the near future.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref19"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref19" class="refNumLink">19</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit19" data-doi="10.1002/anie.201303971"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yuan, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wu, H. A promising family of mixed transition-metal oxides (MTMOs) (designated as AxB3-xO4; A, B=Co, Ni, Zn, Mn, Fe, etc.) with stoichiometric or even non-stoichiometric compns., typically in a spinel structure, has recently attracted increasing research interest worldwide.  Benefiting from their remarkable electrochem. properties, these MTMOs will play significant roles for low-cost and environmentally friendly energy storage/conversion technologies.  In this Review, we summarize recent research advances in the rational design and efficient synthesis of MTMOs with controlled shapes, sizes, compns., and micro-/nanostructures, along with their applications as electrode materials for lithium-ion batteries and electrochem. capacitors, and efficient electrocatalysts for the oxygen redn. reaction in metal-air batteries and fuel cells. Recently, single-phased mixed metal oxides (MMOs) contg. different metal cations, in particular, have confirmed high electrochem. activities because of their complex chem. compn., interfacial effects, and the synergic effects of the multiple metal species.                         cas_cit21R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">21</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Study on the Electrochemical Reaction Mechanism of NiFe2O4 as a High-Performance Anode for Li-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Islam, Mobinul; Ali, Ghulam; Jeong, Min-Gi; Choi, Wonchang; Chung, Kyung Yoon; Jung, Hun-Gi</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ACS Applied Materials &amp; Interfaces</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Nickel ferrite (NiFe2O4) has been previously shown to have a promising electrochem. performance for lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) as an anode material.  However, assocd. electrochem. processes, along with structural changes, during conversion reactions are hardly studied.  Nanocryst. NiFe2O4 was synthesized with the aid of a simple citric acid assisted sol-gel method and tested as a neg. electrode for LIBs.  After 100 cycles at a const. c.d. of 0.5 A g-1 (about a 0.5 C-rate), the synthesized NiFe2O4 electrode provided a stable reversible capacity of 786 mAh g-1 with a capacity retention greater than 85%.  The NiFe2O4 electrode achieved a specific capacity of 365 mAh g-1 when cycled at a c.d. of 10 A g-1 (about a 10 C-rate).  At such a high c.d., this is an outstanding capacity for NiFe2O4 nanoparticles as an anode.  Ex-situ X-ray diffraction (XRD) and X-ray absorption spectroscopy (XAS) were employed at different potential states during the cell operation to elucidate the conversion process of a NiFe2O4 anode and the capacity contribution from either Ni or Fe.  Investigation reveals that the lithium extn. reaction does not fully agree with the previously reported one and is found to be a hindered oxidn. of metallic nickel to nickel oxide in the applied potential window.  Our findings suggest that iron is participating in an electrochem. reaction with full reversibility and forms iron oxide in the fully charged state, while nickel is found to be the cause of partial irreversible capacity where it exists in both metallic nickel and nickel oxide phases.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref22"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref22" class="refNumLink">22</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit22" data-doi="10.1002/cssc.201800579"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Islam, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Jeong, M.-G.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ali, G.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Oh, I.-H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chung, K. Herein, we proposed a rational design of 3D-graphene/CoMoO4 nanoplates by a facile two-step hydrothermal method.  Owing to the high electron transfer rate of graphene and the high activity of the CoMoO4 nanoplates, the three-dimensional electrode architectures achieved remarkable electrochem. performances with high areal specific capacitance (1255.24 F/g at 1 A/g) and superior cycling stability (91.3% of the original specific capacitance after 3000 cycles at 1 A/g).  The all-solid-state asym. supercapacitor composed of 3D-graphene/CoMoO4 and activated carbon (AC) exhibited a specific capacitance of 109 F/g at 0.2 A/g and an excellent cycling stability with only 12.1% of the initial specific capacitance off after 3000 cycles at 2 A/g.         </div><div class="casAbstract">We report the synthesis of porous CoMoO4 nanorods and their applications in lithium oxygen (Li-O2) and lithium ion (Li-ion) batteries.  The unique porous structures of CoMoO4 nanorods can promote the permeation of electrolyte and benefit the transport of lithium ion.  When employed as the cathode catalyst for a Li-O2 battery, CoMoO4 nanorods deliver an improved discharge capacity (4680 mA h g-1), lower charge potential and better cycle stability (41 cycles at 500 mA h g-1 capacity limit) compared with the bare carbon.         </div><div class="casAbstract">A software package for the anal. of x-ray absorption spectroscopy (XAS) data is presented.  This package is based on the IFEFFIT library of numerical and XAS algorithms and is written in the Perl programming language using the Perl/Tk graphics toolkit.  The programs described here are: (i) ATHENA, a program for XAS data processing, (ii) ARTEMIS, a program for EXAFS data anal. using theor. stds. from FEFF and (iii) HEPHAESTUS, a collection of beamline utilities based on tables of at. absorption data.         </div><div class="casAbstract">CoMoO4 nanoplate arrays were grown directly on Ni foam via a template-free hydrothermal route.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Developing earth-abundant catalysts to reduce reliance on noble-metal based ones in sustainable electrochem. processes is nowadays of great interest.  Particularly, the rational structural design of electrocatalysts with high-d. and well-exposed active sites is critically important to reach high catalytic activity.  In this work, we have grown Co9S8 nanowires on carbon fiber paper via a hydrothermal route, and notably the Co9S8 nanowires are encapsulated in an unusual fluffy shell layer constructed using a continuous N,S-codoped carbon coating decorated with copious, vertically grown, short N,S-codoped carbon nanotube material.  Such a nanowire/nanofluff combined structure provides not only many voids for rapid mass transfer, but also numerous sp. surfaces for the loading of active sites, and it can be used well as a bifunctional catalyst in both oxygen evolution and hydrogen evolution reactions.  Particularly, the obtained catalysts exhibit good oxygen evolution activity, showing a small overpotential of 290 mV to obtain a geometric c.d. of 10 mA cm-2 in 1.0 M KOH electrolyte.  Besides, the closely coated carbon layers also successfully prevent the Co9S8 nanowires from dissoln. and recrystn., thus leading favorably to good durability.  Indeed, the reported fluffy carbon shell offers a novel structure design, with distinct advantages regarding the exposure of and access to high-d. active sites, which can be extended to a variety of functional nanomaterials.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref31"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref31" class="refNumLink">31</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit31" data-doi="10.1039/c6cy01378c"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chen, G.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xu, Q.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Song, G.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhao, W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Fan, W.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Solubility Product Difference-Guided Synthesis of Co3O4–CeO2 Core–Shell Catalysts for CO Oxidation</span>. <i>Catal. Systematics in the spin-orbit splittings and binding energies of core levels of the metals are described.  In most of the cases studied, the dependence of the spin-orbit splittings on the at. no. Z is given by the relation ΔE = a(Z-Z0)4, where a is the quantum defect parameter and Z0 is the effective screening.  Core-level binding energies increase with the oxidn. state of the metal.  Most of the core-level binding energies are related to the at. no. Z by the expression E = x(Z-Z0)2, giving rise to linear plots of ln E vs. ln Z.                         cas_cit35R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">35</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Achieving Fully Reversible Conversion in MoO3 for Lithium Ion Batteries by Rational Introduction of CoMoO4</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Wang, Wei; Qin, Jinwen; Yin, Zhigang; Cao, Minhua</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ACS Nano</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Electrode materials based on conversion reactions with lithium ions generally show much higher energy d.  One of the main challenges in the design of these electrode materials is to improve initial Coulombic efficiency and alleviate the vol. changes during the lithiation-delithiation processes.  Here, we achieve fully reversible conversion in MoO3 as an anode for lithium ion batteries by the hybridization of CoMoO4.  The porous MoO3-CoMoO4 microspheres are constructed by homogeneously dispersed MoO3 and CoMoO4 subunits and their lithiation/delithiation processes were studied by ex situ TEM to reveal the mechanism of the reversible conversion reaction.  Co nanoparticles are in situ formed from CoMoO4 during the lithiation process, which then act as the catalyst to guarantee the reversible decompn. of Li2O, thus effectively improving the reversible specific capacity and initial Coulombic efficiency.  Moreover, the pores in MoO3-CoMoO4 microspheres also greatly enhance their mech. strength and provide enough cavity to alleviate vol. changes during repeated cycling.  Such a design concept makes MoO3 to be a potential promising anode in practical applications.  The full cell (LiFePO4 cathode/MoO3-CoMoO4 anode) displays a high capacity up to 155.7 mAh g-1 at 0.1 C and an initial Coulombic efficiency as high as 97.35%.  This work provides impetus for further development in electrochem. charge storage devices.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref36"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref36" class="refNumLink">36</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit36" data-doi="10.1016/j.electacta.2017.10.114"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Islam, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Jeong, M.-G.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hwang, J.-Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Oh, I.-H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Sun, Y.-K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Jung, H.-G.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Self-Assembled Nickel-Cobalt Oxide Microspheres from Rods with Enhanced Electrochemical Performance for Sodium Ion Battery</span>. <i>Electrochim.         </div><div class="casAbstract">A flower-like BaMoO4 composing of numerous microrods has been prepd. via facile hydrothermal synthesis and used as anode materials of lithium ion batteries (LIBs) and sodium ion batteries (SIBs) for the first time.         </div><div class="casAbstract">MoO3/reduced graphene oxide (MoO3/RGO) composites were successfully prepd. via a facile one-step hydrothermal method, and evaluated as anode materials for sodium ion batteries (SIBs).  The crystal structures, morphologies and electrochem. properties of the as-prepd. samples were characterized by X-ray diffraction, field-emission SEM, cyclic voltammetry, electrochem. impedance spectroscopy, and galvanostatic charge/discharge tests, resp.                         cas_cit40R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">40</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Shale-like Co3O4 for high performance lithium/sodium ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Li, Huan-Huan; Li, Zi-Yao; Wu, Xing-Long; Zhang, Lin-Lin; Fan, Chao-Ying; Wang, Hai-Feng; Li, Xiao-Ying; Wang, Kang; Sun, Hai-Zhu; Zhang, Jing-Ping</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Materials Chemistry A: Materials for Energy and Sustainability</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">In recent years, metal-org. compds. have been considered as ideal sacrificial templates to obtain transition metal oxides for electrochem. applications due to their diverse structures and tunable properties.  A new kind of Co-based metal org. compd. with a layered structure was designed and prepd., which was then transformed into ultrafine Co oxide (Co3O4) nanocrystallites via a facile annealing treatment.  The obtained Co3O4 nanocrystallites further assembled into a hierarchical shale-like structure, donating extremely short ion diffusion pathway and rich porosity to the materials.  The special structure largely alleviated the problems of Co3O4 such as inferior intrinsic elec. cond., poor ion transport kinetics and large vol. changes during the redox reactions.  When evaluated as anode materials for Li-ion batteries, the shale-like Co3O4 (S-Co3O4) exhibited superior Li storage properties with a high capacity of 1045.3 mA-h g-1 after 100 cycles at 200 mA g-1 and good rate capabilities up to 10 A g-1.                         cas_cit41R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">41</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Nano Co3O4 as Anode Material for Li-Ion and Na-Ion Batteries: An Insight into Surface Morphology</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Subalakshmi, Palani; Sivashanmugam, Arumugam</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ChemistrySelect</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Co3O4 is regarded as a competent anode material for lithium and sodium ion batteries having high theor. capacity of 890 mAh g-1 obtained through metal displacement reaction with Li or Na.  Nano Co3O4 consisting of 20-30 nm-sized particles was synthesized through a simple pptn. method and characterized through X-ray diffraction, Fourier-transform IR spectroscopy, SEM, Transmission electron microscopy and XPS.  Electrochem. evaluation of the nano Co3O4 as anode material for Li-ion and Na-ion batteries was performed through cyclic voltammetry, electrochem. impedance spectroscopy and charge-discharge cycling studies.  Nano Co3O4 delivered high discharge capacity of 423 mAh g-1 at 0.1 C even after 40 cycles as Li-ion battery anode and exhibited stable discharge behavior up to 130 cycles with high rate capability.  Nano Co3O4 in Na-ion configuration delivered discharge capacity of 101 mAh g-1 at 0.1 C rate even after 30 cycles.  At higher current rates Co3O4 electrodes exhibited severe capacity fading due to the huge vol. change occurred during sodiation / desodiation.  Ex-situ SEM and elemental analyses evidenced that disintegration of electrode structure resulting from vol. change and continuous decompn. of electrolyte were responsible for the poor electrochem. performance of Co3O4 with Na+ ions while in Li-ion configuration nano Co3O4 outshined in cycling performance.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref42"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref42" class="refNumLink">42</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit42" data-doi="10.1039/c5ta02734a"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, L.                         cas_cit42R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">42</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Polycrystalline zinc stannate as an anode material for sodium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Wang, Luyuan Paul; Zhao, Yi; Wei, Chao; Wong, Chuiling; Srinivasan, Madhavi; Xu, Zhichuan J.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Materials Chemistry A: Materials for Energy and Sustainability</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">In this work, we report for the first time the electrochem. performance of polycryst. ZnSnO3 as an anode material for sodium-ion batteries (NIBs).  The as-synthesized cubes were found to be derived from the self-assembly of ZnSnO3 nanoparticles.  A hollow interior was subsequently obtained through a facile etching reaction.  A reversible capacity of 315 mA h g-1 (∼0.3 V vs. Na+/Na) was obtained when cycled between 0.01 and 3.00 V vs. Na+/Na at 30 mA g-1.  A decent capacity retention of 92% was also obsd. after 100 cycles when tested at 250 mA g-1.  The ZnSnO3 hollow cubes were found to demonstrate superior electrochem. performance when compared with both ZnSnO3 solid cubes and manually blended ZnO-SnO2.  The enhanced performance is believed to be attributed to the even distribution of Zn and Sn metallic nanoparticles following the first conversion process, which, conversely, is unachievable in manually blended ZnO-SnO2.  Addnl. free space owing to different vol. expansions during alloying of Zn and Sn easily permits diffusion of Na+ to the interior of the microcube, giving rise to enhanced capacity and cyclability.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref43"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref43" class="refNumLink">43</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit43" data-doi="10.1021/acsami.8b03571"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ali, G.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Islam, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Jung, H.-G.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Nam, K.-W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chung, K.                         cas_cit44R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">44</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Electrochemical Kinetics of the Li[Li0.23Co0.3Mn0.47]O2 Cathode Material Studied by GITT and EIS</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Li, Zhe; Du, Fei; Bie, Xiaofei; Zhang, Dong; Cai, Yongmao; Cui, Xinran; Wang, Chunzhong; Chen, Gang; Wei, Yingjin</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Physical Chemistry C</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">The Li[Li0.23Co0.3Mn0.47]O2 cathode material was prepd. by a sol-gel method.  Combination of x-ray diffraction and Raman scattering studies showed that the material was a solid soln. rather than a composite of nano Li2MnO3 and LiCoO2.  The material had a high discharge capacity of 250 mA-h/g in the voltage window of 2.0-4.8 V.  However, the capacity retention was poor.  The material showed different electrochem. mechanisms in the first charge and subsequent cycles.  Galvanostatic intermittent titrn. technique (GITT) study showed that the Li+ diffusion coeffs. during the first charge were as small as 10-19 cm2/s because of the high kinetic barriers assocd. with the concurrent Li+ extn., oxygen loss, and structural rearrangement.  The Li+ diffusion coeffs. increased to 10-14 cm2/s after the first charge.  However, they were still much smaller than those of typical layered materials such as LiCoO2 and Li(Ni1/3Co1/3Mn1/3)O2.  Electrochem. impedance spectroscopy (EIS) study showed that the large interface impedance at high potential seriously hindered the electrode performance of the material.  A lower charge cutoff voltage of 4.6 V was the most suitable for this material considering that the corresponding reversible capacity (∼200 mA-h/g) was attractive for high energy d. lithium-ion batteries.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref45"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref45" class="refNumLink">45</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit45" data-doi="10.1038/srep42237"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ali, G.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lee, J.-H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chang, W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Cho, B.-W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Jung, H.-G.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Nam, K.-W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chung, K.         </div><div class="casAbstract">The growing demand for lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) requires investigation of high-performance electrode materials with the advantages of being environmentally friendly and cost-effective.  In this study, a nanocomposite of open-pyrochlore-structured FeF3·0.5H2O and reduced graphene oxide (RGO) is synthesized for use as a high-performance cathode in LIBs, where RGO provides high elec. cond. to the composite material. Surprisingly, if the lower cut-off voltage is altered, the appearance of a new charge plateau is seen, with no apparent change in the discharge behavior.  The kinetics of Na insertion and extn. are discussed using CV and EIS techniques.  The authors also report the Na chem. diffusion coeff. of the Na2Ti3O7/CB electrode estd. using GITT.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref49"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref49" class="refNumLink">49</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit49" data-doi="10.1088/0953-8984/12/9/301"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kuzmin, A.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Purans, J.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Dehydration of the Molybdenum Trioxide Hydrates MoO3·nH2O: in Situ X-Ray Absorption Spectroscopy Study at the Mo K Edge</span>. <i>J. Phys.: Condens. Matter</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2000</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>12</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">1959</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1088/0953-8984/12/9/301</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D12%26publication_year%3D2000%26pages%3D1959%26journal%3DJ.%2BPhys.%253A%2BCondens.%2BMatter%26author%3DA.%2BKuzmin%26author%3DJ.%2BPurans%26title%3DDehydration%2Bof%2Bthe%2BMolybdenum%2BTrioxide%2BHydrates%2BMoO3%25C2%25B7nH2O%253A%2Bin%2BSitu%2BX-Ray%2BAbsorption%2BSpectroscopy%2BStudy%2Bat%2Bthe%2BMo%2BK%2BEdge%26doi%3D10.1088%252F0953-8984%252F12%252F9%252F301&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsami.8b16324&amp;doiOfLink=10.1088%2F0953-8984%2F12%2F9%2F301&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit49R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">49</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Dehydration of the molybdenum trioxide hydrates MoO3·nH2O: in situ x-ray absorption spectroscopy study at the Mo K edge</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Kuzmin, Alexei; Purans, Juris</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Physics: Condensed Matter</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2000</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">12</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">9</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">1959-1970</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">JCOMEL</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0953-8984</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Institute of Physics Publishing</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">The process of dehydration for MoO3·2H2O → β-MoO3·H2O → α-MoO3, was studied for the 1st time by in situ Mo K-edge x-ray absorption spectroscopy.  A regularization procedure was applied to reconstruct the radial distribution functions around the Mo at all stages of the process.  Strong variations in the local environment of the Mo were found at both phase transitions and were attributed to the changes in direction of the off-center Mo displacement and in the degree of symmetrization of the Mo-O bonds.  Also, an increase of the Mo-O-Mo angle, leading to the appearance of strong multiple-scattering effects in the x-ray absorption fine structure, was obsd. at the 2nd phase transition β-MoO3·H2O → α-MoO3.  The final α-MoO3 product had nanocryst. structure with an av. grain size of ∼15 Å.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref50"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref50" class="refNumLink">50</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit50" data-doi="10.1016/j.micromeso.2017.01.034"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Jinlong, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Meng, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Suzuki, K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Miura, H.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Synthesis of CoMoO4 @RGO Nanocomposites as High-Performance Supercapacitor Electrodes</span>. <i>Microporous Mesoporous Mater.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2017</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>242</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">264</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">270</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

                        rightTab-cas_cit2R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">2</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">A phosphorene-graphene hybrid material as a high-capacity anode for sodium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Sun, Jie; Lee, Hyun-Wook; Pasta, Mauro; Yuan, Hongtao; Zheng, Guangyuan; Sun, Yongming; Li, Yuzhang; Cui, Yi</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nature Nanotechnology</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Nature Publishing Group</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Na-ion batteries have recently attracted significant attention as an alternative to Li-ion batteries because Na sources do not present the geopolitical issues that Li sources might.  Although recent reports on cathode materials for Na-ion batteries demonstrated performances comparable to their Li-ion counterparts, the major scientific challenge for a competitive Na-ion battery technol. is to develop viable anode materials.  A hybrid material made out of a few phosphorene layers sandwiched between graphene layers shows a specific capacity of 2 440 mA h g-1 (calcd. using the mass of P only) at a c.d. of 0.05 A g-1 and an 83% capacity retention after 100 cycles while operating between 0 and 1.5 V.  Using in situ TEM and ex situ x-ray diffraction techniques, the authors explain the large capacity of the anode through a dual mechanism of intercalation of Na ions along the x axis of the phosphorene layers followed by the formation of a Na3P alloy.  The presence of graphene layers in the hybrid material works as a mech. backbone and an elec. highway, ensuring that a suitable elastic buffer space accommodates the anisotropic expansion of phosphorene layers along the y and z axial directions for stable cycling operation.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref3"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref3" class="rightTabRefNumLink">3</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit3" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.201703268"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Saurel, D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Orayech, B.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xiao, B.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Carriazo, D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Rojo, T.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">From Charge Storage Mechanism to Performance: A Roadmap toward High Specific Energy Sodium-Ion Batteries through Carbon Anode Optimization</span>. <i>Adv.                         rightTab-cas_cit4R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">4</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Elucidating the reaction mechanism of SnF2@C nanocomposite as a high-capacity anode material for Na-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Ali, Ghulam; Lee, Ji-Hoon; Oh, Si Hyoung; Jung, Hun-Gi; Chung, Kyung Yoon</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nano Energy</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Sn-based materials have drawn great attention as anodes for rechargeable batteries because of their extremely high theor. energy storage capacities.  Herein, a nanocomposite based on SnF2 and acetylene black is proposed as a high-performance anode material for sodium-ion batteries and their electrochem. performances, as well as related energy storage mechanism, are investigated.  The nanocomposite electrode delivered a high reversible capacity of 563 mAh g-1 which is considerably improved compared to a reversible capacity of 323 mAh g-1 of the micron-sized bare SnF2 electrode.  The nanocomposite electrode shows superior rate capability and delivers a reversible capacity of 191 mAh g-1 at a high c.d. of 1 C, while the bare electrode delivers negligible capacities.  The changes in crystallog. structure are obsd. using in-situ XRD and the results reveal the existence of a solid soln. of two or more species during dis/charging.  The electronic and at. configurations depending on the state of dis/charging are systematically investigated using ex-situ X-ray absorption spectroscopy.  The results reveal that the valence change of Sn follows the conversion (SnF2 + 2Na → Sn + 2NaF) and alloying (Sn + XNa → SnNaX) reaction upon sodium insertion into a composite.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref5"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref5" class="rightTabRefNumLink">5</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit5" data-doi="10.1039/c2ee02781j"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Palomares, V.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Serras, P.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Villaluenga, I.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hueso, K.             (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Energy prodn. and storage have become key issues concerning our welfare in daily life.  Present challenges for batteries are twofold.  In the first place, the increasing demand for powering systems of portable electronic devices and zero-emission vehicles stimulates research towards high energy and high voltage systems.  In the second place, low cost batteries are required in order to advance towards smart elec. grids that integrate discontinuous energy flow from renewable sources, optimizing the performance of clean energy sources.  Na-ion batteries can be the key for the second point, because of the huge availability of sodium, its low price and the similarity of both Li and Na insertion chemistries.  In spite of the lower energy d. and voltage of Na-ion based technologies, they can be focused on applications where the wt. and footprint requirement is less drastic, such as elec. grid storage.  Much work has to be done in the field of Na-ion in order to catch up with Li-ion technol.  Cathodic and anodic materials must be optimized, and new electrolytes will be the key point for Na-ion success.  This review will gather the up-to-date knowledge about Na-ion battery materials, with the aim of providing a wide view of the systems that have already been explored and a starting point for the new research on this battery technol.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref6"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref6" class="rightTabRefNumLink">6</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit6" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.201200346"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xu, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhu, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, C.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Electrochemical Performance of Porous Carbon/Tin Composite Anodes for Sodium-Ion and Lithium-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>Adv.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Na ion batteries (SIBs) were considered as promising alternative to Li ion batteries (LIBs) for large-scale energy storage.  However, their inferior electrochem. performances, esp. cyclability and rate capability become the major challenge for further development of SIBs.  Herein, Sb/C composite prepd. via an efficient and facile method with alginate as precursor was evaluated as anode material for Na ion batteries.  This method shows unique role in formation of porous Sb/C composite, controlling the particle size and achieving excellent C coating.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Energy-storage devices are state-of-the-art devices with many potential tech. and domestic applications.  Conventionally used batteries do not meet the requirements of elec. or plug-in hybrid-elec. vehicles due to their insufficient energy and power densities.  Graphite is used for the conventional anodes of Li-ion batteries.  However, the specific capacity (372 mA h g-1) of a graphite electrode is not sufficient for high-power applications.  Therefore, Co-, Ni-, Mn-, and Zn-based simple oxides have been investigated as anode components due to their high specific capacities (500-1000 mA h g-1).  Among these, Co-based anodes have demonstrated the best electrochem. performances; however, Co's high cost and toxicity limit its use as an ideal anode component.  Recently, mixed-metal oxides with AB2O4 (A = Cu, Co, Ni, Mn, and Zn; B = Co, Mn, and Fe) and A2BO4 (A = Co, Mn, and Fe; B = Sn, Si, Ti, and Ge) structures have received much interest, and their electrochem. performances have been extensively studied.  This type of mixed-metal oxide affords the following main advantages: they store charge through conversion as well as alloying-dealloying processes, and they exhibit higher electronic conductivities than that obtained with simple metal oxides.  The above points indicate the importance of AB2O4- and A2BO4-structured materials. In this work, we have grown Co9S8 nanowires on carbon fiber paper via a hydrothermal route, and notably the Co9S8 nanowires are encapsulated in an unusual fluffy shell layer constructed using a continuous N,S-codoped carbon coating decorated with copious, vertically grown, short N,S-codoped carbon nanotube material. One of the main challenges in the design of these electrode materials is to improve initial Coulombic efficiency and alleviate the vol. changes during the lithiation-delithiation processes.  Here, we achieve fully reversible conversion in MoO3 as an anode for lithium ion batteries by the hybridization of CoMoO4.  The porous MoO3-CoMoO4 microspheres are constructed by homogeneously dispersed MoO3 and CoMoO4 subunits and their lithiation/delithiation processes were studied by ex situ TEM to reveal the mechanism of the reversible conversion reaction. Moreover, the pores in MoO3-CoMoO4 microspheres also greatly enhance their mech. strength and provide enough cavity to alleviate vol. changes during repeated cycling.  Such a design concept makes MoO3 to be a potential promising anode in practical applications. A</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2013</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>1</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">2653</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">2662</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1039/c2ta01057g</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord" id="
                        rightTab-cas_cit48R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">48</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Na2Ti3O7: an intercalation based anode for sodium-ion battery applications</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Rudola, Ashish; Saravanan, Kuppan; Mason, Chad W.; Balaya, Palani</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Materials Chemistry A: Materials for Energy and Sustainability</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2013</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">1</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">7</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">2653-2662</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">JMCAET</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">2050-7496</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">The authors report here the electrochem. properties of Na2Ti3O7, a potential non-carbon, low-voltage anode material for room temp. Na-ion battery applications.  A solid-state route was used to prep. Na2Ti3O7.  Further, XRD, SEM, TEM, HR-TEM, SAED, XPS and EDX techniques were used to characterize the material.  The Na/Na2Ti3O7 cell displayed a charge capacity of 177 mA h g-1 at 0.1 C rate.  High rate and long term cyclic performance at different rates showed relatively stable storage capacities.  Surprisingly, if the lower cut-off voltage is altered, the appearance of a new charge plateau is seen, with no apparent change in the discharge behavior.  The kinetics of Na insertion and extn. are discussed using CV and EIS techniques.  The authors also report the Na chem. diffusion coeff. of the Na2Ti3O7/CB electrode estd. using GITT.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref49"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref49" class="rightTabRefNumLink">49</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit49" data-doi="10.1088/0953-8984/12/9/301"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kuzmin, A.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Purans, J.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Dehydration of the Molybdenum Trioxide Hydrates MoO3·nH2O: in Situ X-Ray Absorption Spectroscopy Study at the Mo K Edge</span>. <i>J. Phys.: Condens. Matter</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2000</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>12</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">1959</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1088/0953-8984/12/9/301</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord" id="
                        rightTab-cas_cit49R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">49</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Dehydration of the molybdenum trioxide hydrates MoO3·nH2O: in situ x-ray absorption spectroscopy study at the Mo K edge</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Kuzmin, Alexei; Purans, Juris</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Physics: Condensed Matter</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2000</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">12</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">9</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">1959-1970</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">JCOMEL</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0953-8984</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Institute of Physics Publishing</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">The process of dehydration for MoO3·2H2O → β-MoO3·H2O → α-MoO3, was studied for the 1st time by in situ Mo K-edge x-ray absorption spectroscopy.  A regularization procedure was applied to reconstruct the radial distribution functions around the Mo at all stages of the process.  Strong variations in the local environment of the Mo were found at both phase transitions and were attributed to the changes in direction of the off-center Mo displacement and in the degree of symmetrization of the Mo-O bonds.  Also, an increase of the Mo-O-Mo angle, leading to the appearance of strong multiple-scattering effects in the x-ray absorption fine structure, was obsd. at the 2nd phase transition β-MoO3·H2O → α-MoO3.  The final α-MoO3 product had nanocryst. structure with an av. grain size of ∼15 Å.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref50"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref50" class="rightTabRefNumLink">50</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit50" data-doi="10.1016/j.micromeso.2017.01.034"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Jinlong, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Meng, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Suzuki, K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Miura, H.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Synthesis of CoMoO4 @RGO Nanocomposites as High-Performance Supercapacitor Electrodes</span>. <i>Microporous Mesoporous Mater.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2017</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>242</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">264</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">270</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1016/j.micromeso.2017.01.034</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord empty" id="
                            rightTab-cas_R
                        "><div class="casContent">There is no corresponding record for this reference.</div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref51"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref51" class="rightTabRefNumLink">51</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit51" data-doi="10.1007/s11663-016-0816-5"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Cai, D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Sasaki, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ostrovski, O.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Effects of CaO/SiO2 Ratio and Na2O Content on Melting Properties and Viscosity of SiO2-CaO-Al2O3-B2O3-Na2O Mold Fluxes</span>. <i>Metall. Mater. Trans. B</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2016</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>48</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">516</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">526</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1007/s11663-016-0816-5</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord empty" id="
                            rightTab-cas_R
                        "><div class="casContent">There is no corresponding record for this reference.</div></div></div></div></div></li></ol></li></ul></div></div></div></div></article><div class="w-slide article__w-slide">

        <script nonce="9318894558a954b8-SJC">
(function(){
	
	/*Event Tracking for RIS menu dropdown - Article pages*/

	$('.cit-download-dropdown_button').on('click',function(e)
	{	
		e.preventDefault();
	    gtag('event', 'Export', { 'event_category': 'Citation', 'event_label':'Main'});

	});


	$('.cit-download-dropdown_content ul li').on('click',function(e)
	{	
	//	e.preventDefault();
	    var idx = $(this).index();
	    
	    var label = '';
	    if (idx === 0 ) {
	    	label = 'Citation';
	    } else if (idx === 1 ) {
	    	label = 'Abstract';
	    } else if (idx === 2 ) {
	    	label = 'Reference';
	    } else if (idx === 3 ) {
	    	label = 'More Options';
	    }
	   
	   gtag('event', 'Export', { 'event_category': 'Citation', 'event_label':label});
	});

	$('.header_contnav-next a').on('click', function(e){
        e.preventDefault();
        gtag('event', 'Top', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Right Arrow'});
	}); 

 	$('.header_contnav-pre a').on('click', function(e){
	    e.preventDefault();
	     gtag('event', 'Top', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Left Arrow'});
	}); 

	$('.share_button').on('click', function(e){
		e.preventDefault();
		gtag('event', 'Top', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Share'});
	});

	$("#pane-m1con").on('click', function(e) {
		e.preventDefault();
		gtag('event', 'Sidebar', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Figures'});
	});

	$("#pane-m2con").on('click', function(e) {
		e.preventDefault();
	    gtag('event', 'Sidebar', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'References'});
	});

	$("#pane-m3con").on('click', function(e) {
		e.preventDefault();
		gtag('event', 'Sidebar', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Supporting Info'});
	});

	$('.tab__close').on('click', function(){
		gtag('event', 'Sidebar', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Close'});
	}); 



})();

</script>

<script nonce="9318894558a954b8-SJC">
document.addEventListener('DOMContentLoaded', function () {
    const modalContainer = document.querySelector('.my-main-container');

    // Function to show the modal
    function showModal() {
        modalContainer.style.display = 'flex';
        document.querySelector('.overlay').style.display = 'block';
    }

    // Function to hide the modal when the close button is clicked
    function hideModal() {
        modalContainer.style.display = 'none';
        document.querySelector('.overlay').style.display = 'none';
    }

    // Hide the modal initially
    hideModal();

    // Function to handle clicks outside the modal
    function handleClickOutside(event) {
        // const modalContainer = document.querySelector(".my-main-container");
        // Check if the click target is not a child of the modal container
        if (!modalContainer.contains(event.target)) {
            hideModal();
        }
    }

    // Add a click event listener to the close button
    document
        .querySelector('.close-button')
        .addEventListener('click', hideModal);

    // Add a click event listener to the document to handle clicks outside the modal
    document.addEventListener('click', handleClickOutside);

    const screenHeight = window.innerHeight;
    function updateModalPosition() {
        const scrollY = window.scrollY;
        // console.log("scrollY= " + scrollY);
        // modalContainer.style.top = `${50 + scrollY}px`;
        modalContainer.style.top = `${scrollY + screenHeight / 2}px`;
        // console.log("new top= " + modalContainer.style.top);
    }

    // Update the modal position when the user scrolls
    window.addEventListener('scroll', updateModalPosition);

    // Initialize the modal position
    updateModalPosition();

    //---Cookie---
    // Function to set the cookie with a given name, value, and expiration time in days
    function setCookie(name, value, days) {
        var expires = '';
        if (days) {
            var date = new Date();
            date.setTime(date.getTime() + days * 24 * 60 * 60 * 1000);
            expires = '; expires=' + date.toUTCString();
            console.log(expires + 'this is expires dateUTC');
        }
        document.cookie = name + '=' + (value || '') + expires + '; path=/';
        // console.log("document.cookie is: " + document.cookie);
    }

    if (!document.cookie.includes('modalAd')) {
        // Show Modal
        showModal();

        // Set the cookie with the name "modalAd", value "true", and expiration of 3 day
        setCookie('modalAd', 'true', 3);
    } else {
        hideModal();
    }
});

</script>

<script nonce="9318894558a954b8-SJC">
    document.addEventListener("DOMContentLoaded", function(){
    // Function to get a Human readable formatted date
    function getFormattedDateForMaintenance() {
        let today = new Date();
        let dd = String(today.getDate()).padStart(2, '0');
        //January is 0
        let mm = String(today.getMonth() + 1).padStart(2, '0'); 
        let yyyy = today.getFullYear();
        
        let daysOfWeek = ['Sunday', 'Monday', 'Tuesday', 'Wednesday', 'Thursday', 'Friday', 'Saturday'];
        let monthsOfYear = ['January', 'February', 'March', 'April', 'May', 'June', 'July', 'August', 'September', 'October', 'November', 'December'];

        let dayOfWeek = daysOfWeek[today.getDay()];
        let monthOfYear = monthsOfYear[today.getMonth()];

        return dayOfWeek + ', ' + monthOfYear + ' ' + dd + ', ' + yyyy;
    }
    
    // Insert the date into the .system-message-date element
    document.querySelector('.system-message-date').innerText = getFormattedDateForMaintenance();
});
</script>

      var emailValid = "",
        time = 3000,

        errorStatus = false,
        errormessage =
          "<p class='gdpr_err'><b>Please Enter a valid email address<b><p>";

      function init() {
        setTimeout(function () {
          var cookieExist = getCookie("gdpr");
          if (cookieExist) {
            //console.log("gdpr cookie exist");
            return false;
          } else {
            //console.log("gdpr cookie does not exist")
            gdprMessage();
          }
          //Events

          });

      //Show the message
      function gdprMessage() {
        //console.log("Show GDPR Message");
        var tMessage, emailInput, messageButton, ewrap;

        $("#gdpr-message-modal").show();
      }

      function handleClick() {
        //console.log($("#gdpr-input").val());
        emailValid = validateEmail($("#gdpr-input").val());
        if (emailValid) {
          //Submit the form
          //Hide form
          if ($(".gdpr_err")) {
            $(".gdpr_err").hide();
          }

          $("#gdpr-message-content");
          //If successful then...
          setCookie("gdpr", "true");
          //console.log("emailValid " + emailValid + " submiting form");
        } else {
          //Show error message
          gdprErrorMessage();
          //console.log("emailValid " + emailValid);
        }
      }

      function gdprErrorMessage() {
        if (errorStatus) {
          return false;
        } else {
          errorStatus = true;
          $("#gdpr-message-content").append(errormessage);
        }
      }

      function validateEmail(email) {
        var re =
          /^(([^<>()\[\]\\.,;:\s@"]+(\.[^<>()\[\]\\.,;:\s@"]+)*)|(".+"))@((\[[0-9]{1,3}\.[0-9]{1,3}\.[0-9]{1,3}\.[0-9]{1,3}\])|(([a-zA-Z\-0-9]+\.)+[a-zA-Z]{2,}))$/;
        return re.test(String(email).toLowerCase());
      }

      function setCookie(name, value) {
        var expires = "";
        expires = "; expires=Fri, 31 Dec 9999 23:59:59 GMT";
        document.cookie = name + "=" + (value || "") + expires + "; path=/";
      }

      function getCookie(name) {
        var match = document.cookie.match(new RegExp(name + "=([^;]+)"));
        if (match) return match[1];
      }

      function hideForm() {
        //And set cookie
        $("#gdpr-message-modal").hide();
        setCookie("gdpr", "false");
        gtag("event", "GDPR Modal", {
          event_category: "Pubs GDPR",
          event_label: "GDPR Dismissal",
        });
      }

      return {
        init: init,
      };
    })();

    $(document).ready(infoCentralCapture.init);
  </script>
</p>
-->

                    
                        



        
        <script nonce="9318894558a954b8-SJC">
var currentJournal = "ACS Applied Materials &amp;amp; Interfaces";
$(".citationLinks > a").click(function(e){
    if($(this).text() === "ACS Full Text ") {
        gtag('event', 'ACS Full Text', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
    else if($(this).text() === "Crossref") {
        gtag('event', 'Crossref', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
    else if($(this).text() === "CAS") {
        gtag('event', 'CAS', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
    else if($(this).text() === "PubMed") {
        gtag('event', 'PubMed', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
    else if($(this).text() === "Google Scholar") {
        gtag('event', 'Google Scholar', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
    else if($(this).hasClass("issue-item_open-url") === true) {
        gtag('event', 'OpenURL', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
});
</script>
